                        PageBuilder-pageRow
                        
                      "><div class="
                              PageBuilder-col-12 PageBuilder-col
                               PageBuilder-article
                            "><div id="ArticleHeader-3"><div><header class="ArticleHeader-articleHeader" id="main-article-header"><div class="ArticleHeader-wrapperHeroNoImage ArticleHeader-wrapperHero ArticleHeader-wrapper ArticleHeader-wrapperNoImage"><a href="https://www.cnbc.com/politics/" alt="" class="ArticleHeader-eyebrow" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">Politics</a><h1 class="ArticleHeader-headline">The farm bill&#x27;s failure is a showcase of a fractious, polarized political system</h1><div class="ArticleHeader-time"><time>Published Fri, May 18 2018 • 4:33 PM EDT</time><span> <span class="ArticleHeader-cnbcDivider">|</span> <time> <!-- -->Updated Fri, May 18 2018 • 6:47 PM EDT</time></span></div></div><div class="ArticleHeader-authorAndShare"><div class="ArticleHeader-authorContainer"><div class="ArticleHeader-author"><div class="Author-author"><div class="Author-authorInfo"><div class="Author-authorPortrait" style="background-image:url(https://image.cnbcfm.com/api/v1/image/104239559-HARWOOD_J_8x10.jpg?v=1503503499&amp;w=60&amp;h=60)"></div><div class="Author-authorNameAndSocial"><a href="https://www.cnbc.com/john-harwood/" alt="" class="Author-authorName" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">John Harwood</a><a href="https://twitter.com/johnjharwood" alt="" class="Author-authorTwitter" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">@johnjharwood</a></div></div></div></div></div><div><div class="Share-share" id="social-buttons"></div></div></div></header></div></div></div></div><div class="
                        PageBuilder-pageRow
                        
                      "><div class="
                              PageBuilder-col-8 PageBuilder-col
                               PageBuilder-article
                            "><div class="KeyPoints-keyPoints" id="KeyPoints-5"><div class="KeyPoints-wrapper" style="border-top-color:#002f6c"><div class="KeyPoints-header">Key Points</div><div class="KeyPoints-list"><div><div class="group"><ul><li>The massive 600-page legislation flunked the first big test of whether Congress can sustain the bipartisan coalition undergirding one of America&#x27;s 20 largest industries.</li><li>Since Congress first offered major support for agriculture during the Great Depression, farm assistance programs have faced a recurrent challenge in preserving political support.</li><li>The failure means that the Senate farm bill won&#x27;t include the sorts of food stamp restrictions that helped sink the House proposal Friday</li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="ArticleBody-articleBody" id="ArticleBody-6" data-module="ArticleBody"><span class="HighlightShare-hidden" style="top:0;left:0"></span><div class="InlineImage-imageEmbed" id="ArticleBody-InlineImage-103414925"><div class="InlineImage-wrapper"><div class="InlineImage-imagePlaceholder" style="padding-bottom:63.63333333333333%"><div><div style="height:100%" class="lazyload-placeholder"></div></div></div><div><div class="InlineImage-imageEmbedCaption">A farmer harvests corn near Burlington, Iowa.</div><div class="InlineImage-imageEmbedCredit">Getty Images</div></div></div></div><div class="group"><p>For 21st century Washington, governing is a climb that keeps getting steeper. Consider the <a href="https://www.cnbc.com/2018/05/18/house-farm-bill-vote-immigration-spat-sinks-legislation.html" alt="" class="" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">farm bill defeated on the House floor Friday</a>.</p><p>As dramas in the capital go, this one is distinctly second-tier. But at the hands of both Democrats and Republicans, this massive 600-page legislation flunked the first big test of whether Congress can sustain the bipartisan coalition undergirding one of America&#x27;s 20 largest industries.</p><div class="BoxInline-container"><div id="BoxInline-ArticleBody-6" class="BoxInline-container"></div></div><p>Since Congress first offered major support for agriculture during the Great Depression, farm assistance programs have faced a recurrent challenge in preserving political support. One in four Americans were involved in the industry then, but that has now shrunk to one in fifty.</p><p>One modern breakthrough took place in the late 1970s, thanks to two farm-state senators. Democrat George McGovern of South Dakota and Republican Bob Dole of Kansas – each at different points his party&#x27;s nominee for president – teamed up on a significant expansion in the food stamp program.</p></div><div role="button" tabindex="0" id="Placeholder-ArticleBody-Video-105217978" class="PlaceHolder-wrapper vilynx_enabled" data-vilynx-id="7000020421"><div class="InlineVideo-videoEmbed" id="InlineVideo-0"><div class="InlineVideo-wrapper"><div style="height:100%" class="lazyload-placeholder"></div><div class="PlayButton-container"><div class="PlayButton-featured PlayButton-base" data-type="cnbc"><span class="PlayButton-flyout">watch now</span><span class="icon-play-triangle PlayButton-icon"></span></div></div><div class="InlineVideo-videoFooter"><div class="InlineVideo-videoDurationContainer"><span class="InlineVideo-videoLabel">VIDEO</span><span class="InlineVideo-videoDuration InlineVideo-videoLabel">01:54</span></div><div class="InlineVideo-title">Farm Bill fails in the house</div></div></div></div><div class="PlaceHolder-endCard PlaceHolder-inactive"></div></div><div class="group"><p>They firmly linked two disparate constituencies: conservatives representing farmers in Midwest and Plains states, and liberals representing low-income Americans elsewhere, especially in big cities. That&#x27;s been enough to pass an updated version of the farm bill every few years ever since.</p><p>But the shifting winds of a fractious, polarized political system have rattled that consensus in several ways.</p><p>First, the increasing concentration of farm production and profits into a diminishing number of large agri-businesses have left federal subsidies for commodity prices, conservation and crop insurance more vulnerable to political attacks. In some cases that produces bipartisan opposition – as in an unsuccessful attempt on the House floor this week to curb subsidies for the sugar industry.</p><div id="MidResponsive-ArticleBody-6" class=""></div><p>The dominance of Big Agriculture also makes it easier for House Democrats to defect over issues closer to home. Far more than in Dole&#x27;s era, today&#x27;s GOP focuses consistently on cutting federal benefits for low-income Americans.</p></div><div class="InlineImage-imageEmbed" id="ArticleBody-InlineImage-104907389"><div class="InlineImage-wrapper"><div class="InlineImage-imagePlaceholder" style="padding-bottom:66.64999999999999%"><div><div style="height:100%" class="lazyload-placeholder"></div></div></div><div><div class="InlineImage-imageEmbedCaption">Speaker of the House Paul Ryan, accompanied by Rep. Kevin McCarthy (R-CA), and Rep. Steve Scalise (R-LA), speaks at a news conference following a closed House Republican conference meeting on Capitol Hill in Washington, U.S., December 19, 2017.</div><div class="InlineImage-imageEmbedCredit">Aaron P. Bernstein | Reuters</div></div></div></div><div class="group"><p>So Democrats rebelled en masse against this farm bill&#x27;s new work requirements for food stamp recipients. Complaining that more than 1 million households would lose food stamp benefits, all 183 House Democrats on hand voted no.</p><p>That defeated the bill because 30 House Republicans, defying lame-duck Speaker <a href="https://www.cnbc.com/paul-ryan/" alt="" class="" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">Paul Ryan</a> and other party leaders, joined Democrats for radically different reasons. Their no votes reflected <a href="https://www.cnbc.com/2018/05/18/house-gop-leaders-face-immigration-and-farm-bill-backlash.html" alt="" class="" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">the the Trump-era centrality of restricting immigration</a>.</p><p>As Democrats and GOP moderates seek separate legislation to protect so-called immigrant  &quot;Dreamers,&quot; members of the conservative House Freedom Caucus decided the farm bill could become a bargaining chip to block them. Unable to gain the concessions they wanted, enough caucus members to sink the bill withheld their votes.</p><p>Farm bills have failed before. Congress couldn&#x27;t pass one on schedule in 2012, leading lawmakers to extend provisions of the 2008 version. The current law, which finally passed in 2014, is projected to cost $455 billion by its scheduled expiration later this year.</p><p>With 2018 midterm elections looming, Congress could fall back on a similar stop-gap extension this year. But the Senate isn&#x27;t giving up.</p><p>As a senior member of the Agriculture Committee, Senate Majority Leader <a href="https://www.cnbc.com/mitch-mcconnell/" alt="" class="" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">Mitch McConnell</a> of Kentucky takes a strong personal interest in the issue. And because the farm bill won&#x27;t be considered under special  &quot;reconciliation&quot; rules requiring only a majority vote, Senate passage will require support from at least nine Democrats to surmount an opposition filibuster.</p><p>That assures the Senate farm bill won&#x27;t include the sorts of food stamp restrictions that helped sink the House proposal today. Which means, in turn, that Congress will have at least one more chance to patch together a bipartisan farm coalition once again.</p></div><div class="ArticleBody-subtitle">WATCH: GOP leaders set back as Farm Bill fails</div><div class="group"></div><div role="button" tabindex="0" id="Placeholder-ArticleBody-Video-105217875" class="PlaceHolder-wrapper vilynx_enabled" data-vilynx-id="7000020405"><div class="InlineVideo-videoEmbed" id="InlineVideo-0"><div class="InlineVideo-wrapper"><div style="height:100%" class="lazyload-placeholder"></div><div class="PlayButton-container"><div class="PlayButton-featured PlayButton-base" data-type="cnbc"><span class="PlayButton-flyout">watch now</span><span class="icon-play-triangle PlayButton-icon"></span></div></div><div class="InlineVideo-videoFooter"><div class="InlineVideo-videoDurationContainer"><span class="InlineVideo-videoLabel">VIDEO</span><span class="InlineVideo-videoDuration InlineVideo-videoLabel">01:08</span></div><div class="InlineVideo-title">GOP leaders set back as Farm Bill fails to pass by vote of 198-213</div></div></div></div><div class="PlaceHolder-endCard PlaceHolder-inactive"></div></div><div class="group"></div><div id="ArticleBody-MobileAdhesion" class="MobileAdhesion-container"></div></div><div             Card-placeholder
          "><div style="height:100%" class="lazyload-placeholder"></div></div></div></a><div class="Card-textContent"><div class="Card-titleAndFooter"><div class=""><div class="Card-titleContainer"><a href="https://www.cnbc.com/2018/12/21/government-shutdown-starts-as-trump-and-congress-fail-to-strike-deal-on-border-wall.html" alt="" class="Card-title" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">US government shutdown appears set to continue until Thursday as fight over Trump&#x27;s border wall stalls spending talks</a></div></div><div class="Card-cardFooter "><span class="Card-byline"><a href="https://www.cnbc.com/jacob-pramuk/" alt="" class="" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label="">Jacob Pramuk</a></span></div></div></div></div></div><div class="Column-squareLeadRight "><div class="Card-standardBreakerCard Card-rectangleToRectangleMediaHalf Card-card"><a href="https://www.cnbc.com/2018/12/21/mattis-resignation-letter-is-must-read-warning-about-the-future.html" alt="" class="Card-mediaContainer" target="" title="" role="" tabindex="" data-type="" aria-label=""><div class=""><div class="
